    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="6fd5bf49096a49239b0d200137aad375">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '513b7ef5-cc58-48a8-ad80-c2e8b1106a55'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = 'F89C36C4BBA8C7348A2AB8E2D4EAAE3F';

                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else

               <p>Institute of Physics, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Beijing, 100190 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Bai/Xuedong">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Seok+Park/Ho" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="am9" data-db-target-for="am9" aria-controls="am9" aria-haspopup="true" id="am9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Prof. Ho Seok Park<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="am9" aria-labelledby="am9_Ctrl" role="region" id="am9">

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d39831927" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Porous amorphous carbon nanofiber embedded with Bi nanoparticles in the void spaces (Bi@NC) serves as a core–shell structured Na metal host. The <i>in-situ</i> formed Na<sub>3</sub>Bi facilitates rapid nucleation and ion diffusion, enabling uniform Na plating within the cavities and a stable SEI outside the carbon nanofiber. This design ensures high-rate performance and cycle stability, thereby providing a testament to its suitability in Na metal anode chemistry.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/be926739-08af-4cff-a8d8-d7ec221614bc/anie202417930-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/be926739-08af-4cff-a8d8-d7ec221614bc/anie202417930-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f8bbff05-f565-4c1c-83a1-0d57c9175176/anie202417930-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/be926739-08af-4cff-a8d8-d7ec221614bc/anie202417930-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d39831930" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium metal anodes (SMA), featuring high energy content, low electrochemical potential and easy availability, are a compelling option for sustainable energy storage. However, notorious sodium dendrite and unstable solid-electrolyte interface (SEI) have largely retarded their widespread implantation. Herein, porous amorphous carbon nanofiber embedded with Bi nanoparticles in nanopores (Bi@NC) was rationally designed as a 3D host for SMA. <i>In situ</i> and <i>ex situ</i> characterizations, along with theoretical simulations unlock that the <i>in-situ</i> formed Na−Bi alloy significantly accelerates sodium metal nucleation and sodium ion diffusion kinetics, enabling uniform sodium plating within the void spaces and a stable SEI outside the carbon nanofiber. Particularly, the Bi@NC electrode achieved a high coulombic efficiency of 99.99 % at 3 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> in half-cell tests, a cycle life of 1000 hours at 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, and sustained performance over 600 cycles under harsh conditions under 30 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> within symmetrical cells. The full battery assembled with a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C cathode and Bi@NC anode delivered long-term cyclability over 800 cycles, demonstrating its potential for flexible application of sodium-based energy storage systems. This work highlights the Bi@NC electrode as a promising candidate for high-performance and flexible sodium metal batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417930-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417930-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Exploring new secondary battery systems, such as sodium (Na) ion batteries (SIBs) and Na metal batteries (SMBs) is imperative for replacing Li-ion batteries for sustainable society. In particular, the Na metal anode (SMA) of SMBs is very attractive owing to its high theoretical specific capacity (1166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), low electrochemical potential (−2.714 V vs. SHE), natural abundance, and cost-effectiveness.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0001_R_d39831919e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span> However, the practical application of SMA is limited by the dendrite formation, repetitive solid-electrolyte interface (SEI) destruction and reconstruction, and unwanted parasitic reactions with the electrolyte, which result in a rapid capacity degradation, low coulombic efficiency (CE), and safety concern.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0002_R_d39831919e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In order to resolve these issues, various chemical strategies have been adopted, these including three-dimensional (3D) Na metal hosts,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0005_R_d39831919e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> artificial SEIs,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0009_R_d39831919e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span> and optimized electrolytes and additives.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0010_R_d39831919e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> In particular, designing core–shell structures with rich sodiophilic nucleation sites is expected to construct advanced Na metal hosts for advanced SMA. For instance, the sodiophilic core acts as a heterogeneous seed, resulting in a spatial nucleation control and uniform Na metal deposition, while the confined amorphous carbon shell serves as a protective layer for the improved reversibility.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0013_R_d39831919e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a></sup></span> This structural design can alleviate volume variations during Na metal plating and stripping, suppress parasitic reactions, and guide uniform Na deposition. Accordingly, high CE and improved cycling stability of SMA have been achieved by adopting hollow amorphous carbon shells combined with various heterogeneous seeds in the cores, such as N-doped carbon,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0014_R_d39831919e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> Au nanoparticles,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0017_R_d39831919e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy">8</a></sup></span> SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0018_R_d39831919e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Joule">9</a></sup></span> CoSe<sub>2</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0019_R_d39831919e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Power Sources">10</a></sup></span> Sb nanoparticles,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0020_R_d39831919e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> atomic Sn,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0023_R_d39831919e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> and Zn.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0026_R_d39831919e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span> Despite these advancements, a long-term cyclability of SMA at elevated capacities and densities still remains a notable challenge.</p>
         
         <p>The development of 3D core–shell structured Na hosts, which combine structural integrity with efficient charge transporting capability, has shown potential in resolving the afore-mentioned issues.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0027_R_d39831919e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span> However, the fabrication complexity of these hosts limits their wider adoption. In particular, electrospinning is regarded as an economically attractive and controllable fabrication method to synthesize 3D porous structures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0028_R_d39831919e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Energy Chem.">15</a></sup></span> Bismuth (Bi) is featured with its high Na affinity and rapid Na ion diffusion kinetics for efficient and dendrite-free cycling of SMA.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0029_R_d39831919e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></sup></span> Moreover, the cost-effectiveness of Bi is beneficial for a large-scale deployment.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0032_R_d39831919e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17">17</a></sup></span> Although the electrospun Bi-embedded fibers has been investigated as alloying anode materials of SIBs,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0035_R_d39831919e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Res.">18</a></sup></span> the 3D Bi core-carbon shell structure for applications into a host of SMA has yet to be explored. Furthermore, the confined space provided by the core–shell structure for Na deposition is postulated to promote Na ion diffusion within the Na−Bi alloy, ultimately enhancing the reversible and stable Na deposition.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we report the synthesis of porous amorphous carbon fiber embedded with Bi nanoparticles in the void space (Bi@NC) via electrospinning technology for practical SMAs. Comprehensive characterizations and theoretical simulations decipher that Na−Bi alloy enhances nucleation kinetics and ion diffusion for high-rate capability and reversibility. The confinement of Na deposition in the void spaces within Bi@NC inhibits dendrites, alleviates stress from volume variations, and stabilizes the SEI, leading to superior cycling durability. The full batteries paired with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (NVP@C) cathode achieved a high CE of 99.99 % over 2700 hours and a high capacity of 90.45 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 800 cycles, showcasing its potential for advanced SMBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417930-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417930-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The 3D self-supported Bi@NC fibers were synthesized using the electrospinning technology (Figure S1). The Bi@NC paper is enough flexible and mechanically robust to achieve exceptional resistance to twisting, folding, and bending deformations (Figure S2). Bi atoms are migrated onto the Bi/C interface for the minimization of surface energy, leading to the formation of Bi nanoparticles confined within the void spaces of porous carbon fibers during a high-temperature carbonization.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0036_R_d39831919e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span> The uniform dispersion of Bi nanoparticles within the void spaces of these carbon nanofibers is beneficial for the 3D host of SMA, because they act as efficient nucleation sites of Na metals by forming a Na- Bi alloy with high adsorption energy, effectively directing sodium deposition into these nanopores. This preferential deposition is further influenced by the principles of surface curvature and energy minimization.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0016_R_d39831919e641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7b - ACS Energy Lett.">7b</a></sup></span> Specifically, the inner surfaces of the nanopores, featuring a negative curvature, are more energetically favorable for sodium plating compared to the positively curved outer surface of the carbon fiber. Moreover, the formation of Na−Bi alloys accelerates the nucleation kinetics of Na metal, facilitates the dynamics of the initial deposition process, and lowers the energy barrier for Na ion diffusion, which can greatly improve the deposition reversibility and kinetics of SMA at the high current densities. The Bi nanoparticles are embedded and confined inside the 3D porous carbon nanofibers, and the Na ions are able to be diffused through the amorphous carbon walls and migrated into the inner space of porous Bi@NC via an interfacial diffusion mechanism. This feature of Bi@NC leads to guide a homogeneous Na deposition within the Bi@NC fibers for the inhibition of dendrite formation, to delocalize stress on volume variation, and to stabilize SEIs as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0001">1a</a>. By contrast, Na metals are prone to be unevenly nucleated and deposited on the surface of NC fibers due to the absence of Bi nanoparticles, which results in the poor reversibility of Na metal deposition and stripping.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417930-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ebeecd1d-f37c-4eb0-9dba-9445712c8812/anie202417930-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ebeecd1d-f37c-4eb0-9dba-9445712c8812/anie202417930-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4b8fe0d5-a931-4d23-8d01-3040d6912842/anie202417930-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ebeecd1d-f37c-4eb0-9dba-9445712c8812/anie202417930-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417930-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417930" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Schematic illustration about the comparison of Na deposition on NC and Bi@NC hosts. b, c) SEM, d) TEM, e) HAADF-STEM, and f) HRTEM images of Bi@NC. g) HAADF-STEM and EDS elemental mapping images of Bi, C and N in Bi@NC. h) XRD patterns, i) Bi 4 f XPS spectrum of Bi@NC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The morphology and microstructure of Bi@NC were characterized using Scanning Electron Microscopy (SEM) and Transmission Electron Microscopy (TEM). The Bi@NC and NC are composed of porous carbon fibers with a porosity of 62.68 % (Figures <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0001">1b</a>–<a href="#anie202417930-fig-0001">1d</a> and Figures S3–S5). The abundant nanopores were introduced by the removal of polymethyl methacrylate (PMMA) during the high-temperature carbonization process. As shown in high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) images in Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0001">1e</a>, the Bi particles exhibit a non-spherical, irregular sheet-like morphology, in a size range from 3 to 20 nanometers. They are encapsulated within the nanopores, exhibiting the unique core–shell structure. The lattice fringe of the Bi@NC fibers is estimated as 0.33 nm corresponding to the (012) crystalline plane of Bi as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0001">1f</a>. Notably, the HAADF-STEM and the corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping of Bi@NC fibers confirmed the uniform distribution of C, N, and Bi elements (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0001">1g</a>). Thermogravimetric Analysis (TGA) result indicated the content of Bi in the Bi@NC electrode is approximately 2.49 % by weight (Figure S6). The Bi nanoparticles within the hierarchical porous structures provides large accessible sodiophilic sites and facilitate the spatial deposition within the confined environment. This hierarchical structure of the Bi@NC enables to achieve efficient electrolyte infiltration and accessibility of Na<sup>+</sup> ions to nucleation sites for the improved wettability (Figure S7). The crystalline structure of Bi@NC and NC fibers was also analyzed by collecting the XRD patterns (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0001">1h</a>). Obviously, the distinct XRD peaks of Bi@NC are observed at 23.1°, 28.0°, 37.7°, 40.8°, and 49.0°, which are assigned to the (113), (024), (125), (208), and (134) crystalline planes of rhombohedral Bi structure (JCPDS No.01–0699), respectively. <sup>[20]</sup> This finding indicates the successful conversion from BiCl<sub>3</sub> to Bi. The broad peaks at 26° and 43° originate from the carbon fiber. As analyzed by the N<sub>2</sub> sorption/desorption isotherms, the Bi@NC and NC papers exhibit the specific surface areas of 324 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and 595 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, along with average pore sizes of 7.25 nm and 8.07 nm (Figures S8 and S9). The presence of Bi nanoparticles for the Bi@NC decreases the specific surface area presumably due to the partial blocking of the mesopores. The chemical structures of Bi@NC fibers are further characterized using the spectroscopic methods. The Raman spectra reveal a higher I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio for Bi@NC fibers (1.2) compared to the NC fibers (1.04) (Figure S10). The elevated ratio of Bi@NC is indicative of a more disordered carbon matrix due to the existence of Bi nanoparticles, which offer abundant active sites for Na plating.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0038_R_d39831919e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small">21</a></sup></span> XPS was applied to reveal the chemical valence states of Bi@NC and NC fibers (Figure S11a). The Bi 4f spectrum exhibited Bi 4f <sub>7/2</sub> and 4f <sub>5/2</sub> peaks of at 159.0 and 164.3 eV, respectively, confirming the presence of Bi (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0001">1i</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0039_R_d39831919e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Alloys Compd.">22</a></sup></span> In the C1s spectrum, three peaks at 285.2, 286.6 and 289.7 eV are assigned to C−C, C−O, and O−C=O bonds, respectively (Figure S11b). The deconvoluted N 1s spectrum shows peaks at 398.3, 400.2, and 401.7 eV, corresponding to pyridinic N, pyrrolic N, and graphitic N), respectively (Figure S11c). The XPS depth profiling of the Bi@NC structure confirms a higher concentration of Bi nanoparticles within the carbon fibers rather than on the surface (Figure S12 and S13).</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performances of Bi@NC, NC, and Cu electrodes in accommodating Na metal were systematically evaluated by galvanostatic cycling tests. The Na||Bi@NC half-cell demonstrates superior cycle stability with a high average CE of 99.85 % for over 1500 cycles at a current density of 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and a specific capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure S14). The Na||NC electrode has fluctuated profile with the CE of 98.81 % after 1036 cycles, while the Na||Cu electrode demonstrates obvious undulation with the CE of 89.79 % after only 61 cycles. When the current density and areal capacity increased to 3 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the Na||Bi@NC half-cell can still sustain a high CE of 99.99 % after over 1000 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2a</a>), demonstrating a low hysteresis of 31 mV in the plating/stripping voltage profiles (Figure S15). In contrast, the Na||NC endured an unstable cycling and experienced a short circuit after 700 cycles with an inferior average CE of 99.20 %, while the Na||Cu exhibits significant voltage fluctuations with an average CE of 85.65 % and experienced failure after only 70 cycles, which was attributed to the irreversible side reaction and uncontrollable growth of Na dendrites. The rate performances of three electrodes were measured varying current densities ranging from 0.5 to 5.0 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2b</a>). The rate capability of Bi@NC electrode was better than Cu, NC electrodes. As depicted in Figures <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2c</a>–<a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2e</a>, the nucleation overpotentials of the NC and Bi@NC electrodes are much lower than that of the Cu electrode. By stark contrast, the Cu electrode exhibited significantly higher overpotentials and even encountered a short circuit at the elevated current density of 5.0 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure S16). At current densities of 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 4.0, and 5.0 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, the nucleation overpotentials for Bi@NC gradually increased from 10.41 mV to 14.34, 16.46, 18.55, and 19.79 mV, respectively. In comparison, the NC electrode displayed a more rapid increase in nucleation overpotentials, ranging from 11.01 to 29.71 mV at current densities from 0.5 to 5.0 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. Compared to other electrospun carbon fiber counterparts previously reported (Figure S17, Tables S1),<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0040_R_d39831919e759" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span> the Bi@NC electrode demonstrates higher CE and longer cyclic stability, highlighting its superiority. The interfacial stability and charge transfer kinetics were assessed using electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). The Nyquist plots and corresponding fitted curves of the Bi@NC, NC, and Cu electrodes were presented before cycling and after the initial cycle and 10 cycles at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> in Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2f</a> and Figures S18 and S19. Fitting the experimental data with an equivalent circuit<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0049_R_d39831919e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span> (inset in Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2f</a>), the simulated values of each component were summarized in Table S2. The fresh Bi@NC electrode exhibited a lower charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of 8.42 Ω compared to those of the NC (13.83 Ω) and Cu (397.50 Ω) electrodes. Even after the initial cycle and 10 cycles, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of the Bi@NC electrode was 6.35 Ω, lower than those of the NC (11.26 Ω) and Cu (244.50 Ω) electrodes. The low R<sub>SEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of Bi@NC electrode highlight its superior interfacial kinetics, which contribute to the improved electrochemical performance.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417930-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c129fba3-10b7-4425-9034-dc614e4f9b65/anie202417930-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c129fba3-10b7-4425-9034-dc614e4f9b65/anie202417930-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6b8cb165-6128-4949-b357-d5600d247667/anie202417930-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c129fba3-10b7-4425-9034-dc614e4f9b65/anie202417930-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417930-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417930" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) CEs of the Cu, NC, and Bi@NC electrodes at 3 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. (b) Rate performances of the Cu, NC, and Bi@NC electrodes at various current densities. Voltage profiles of (c) NC and (d) Bi@NC electrodes at various current densities. (e) Nucleation overpotentials of the Cu, NC, and Bi@NC electrodes at various current densities. (f) Nyquist plots and fitted curves (solid line) of Bi@NC (red) and NC (blue) electrodes after 1st (hollow dots) and 10th cycles (solid dots). Galvanostatic discharge/charge potential profiles in symmetric cells for Bi/NC@Na||Bi/NC@Na at (g) 3 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, (h) 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, and (i) 30 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. (j) Rate performance in symmetric cells for the Na||Na, NC@Na||NC@Na, and Bi/NC@Na||Bi/NC@Na electrodes at various current densities. (k) Tafel curves of Cu, NC and Bi@NC electrodes. (l) Comparison between the electrochemical performance in symmetric cells of various carbon hosts for Na metal.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The symmetric cells configurations were constructed to further evaluate their electrochemical performances. At a low current density of 3 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2g</a>), the Bi@NC||Bi@NC symmetric cell shows a consistent voltage profile over 2700 hours along with a minimal voltage hysteresis of 16 mV. On the other hand, the NC||NC symmetric cell exhibits erratic voltage fluctuations and a noticeable increase in voltage hysteresis after 800 hours, while the Na||Na symmetric cell displays a sudden spike in voltage hysteresis leading to short circuits after 150 hours. The increased voltage hysteresis was attributed to the thickening SEI film and the formation of dead Na during the long-term cycling. While increasing the current density to 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2h</a>), the symmetric cell with Bi@NC electrode maintained its low voltage hysteresis and stable voltage profiles over 1000 hours. By contrast, the symmetric cell with NC electrode showed fluctuating voltage profiles with larger voltage hysteresis and a reduced cell lifespan of 250 hours, while the symmetric cell with pure Na metal failed rapidly after 65 hours (Figure S20). Under more severe condition of 30 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the Bi@NC||Bi@NC symmetric cell achieved a significantly lower voltage hysteresis of 59 mV and extended lifetime of 125 hours (or 630 cycles), far exceeding those of the NC||NC and Na||Na counterparts (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2i</a> and Figure S21). The rate performances of three symmetric cells were evaluated varying current densities from 0.5 to 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2j</a>). Among three electrodes, the Bi@NC||Bi@NC symmetric cell achieved the lowest voltage polarization and the capability to smoothly return to the initial state at 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, indicating good rate performance. The exchange current densities derived from the Tafel profiles are 0.89, 1.79, and 4.69 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for Na, NC, and Bi@NC electrodes, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2k</a>). The higher exchange current density of Bi@NC highlights its fast Na deposition kinetics (Figure S22). In particular, the superiority of the Bi@NC electrode is confirmed by comparing the electrochemical performances of the symmetric cells with the carbon-based hosts including sodiophilic sites<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0027_R_d39831919e867" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202417930-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0040_R_d39831919e867" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a>, <a href="#anie202417930-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0052_R_d39831919e867" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25">25</a></sup></span> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0002">2l</a> and Table S3). Consequently, the Bi@NC electrode exhibits a long-term cycle stability at ultrahigh current density, underscoring its advantages over other carbon hosts counterparts.</p>
         
         <p>The Na metal deposition behavior of Bi@NC electrodes is subsequently explored using <i>in-situ</i> optical microscopy and <i>ex-situ</i> SEM. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0003">3a</a>, Cu substrate exhibited the rapid formation of Na dendrites within the initial 15 minutes and the continuous growth over time. The NC substrate showed initial Na accumulation at the 15-minute mark, as followed by nascent detectable dendrite formation after 90 minutes. Notably, the Bi@NC substrate was free from dendrite formation up to the 90-minute mark, which indicates a uniform Na deposition process. Subsequent <i>ex-situ</i> SEM analysis was applied to investigate elaborate morphological evolution of Bi@NC fibers, NC, and Cu substrates during the Na plating/stripping process. Upon Na plating at a specific capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the Bi@NC substrate displayed minimal morphological changes, suggesting the effective encapsulation of Na within the void spaces and an improved Na accommodation for high capacity (Figure S23). On the other hand, the pronounced external Na plating was observed on the NC fiber (Figure S24), while a disordered Na distribution was observed for the Cu substrate under identical plating conditions (Figure S25). While increasing the deposition capacity to 2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the morphology of Bi@NC fiber was preserved without apparent Na metal bumps and dendrite proliferation (Figure S23c), which implies the homogeneous and stable Na plating. On the other hand, the NC fibers demonstrated uneven Na deposition (Figure S24c), while the Cu substrate exhibited distinct mossy Na aggregations (Figure S25c). As the capacity increased to 2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> in the stripping process, the fibrous morphology of Bi@NC was recovered, signifying exceptional reversibility (Figures S23d and S23e). On the other hand, the NC substrate retained uneven Na agglomerates (Figures S24d and S24e), while the Cu substrate displayed substantial residual Na with the lowest reversibility (Figures S25d and S25e).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417930-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8fdaff80-b671-4f25-b757-ef66f78fdef5/anie202417930-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8fdaff80-b671-4f25-b757-ef66f78fdef5/anie202417930-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bf3168be-fb77-43fd-90f9-eaa68a06c879/anie202417930-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8fdaff80-b671-4f25-b757-ef66f78fdef5/anie202417930-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417930-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417930" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) <i>In-situ</i> optical photographs of Na deposition on Cu, NC, and Bi@NC at a current density of 1.0 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) F 1s, C 1s, and Na 1s XPS spectra of Bi@NC and NC electrodes after 10 cycles. c) The Cryo-TEM image of SEI in Bi@NC and d) NC electrodes after 1 cycle and 10 cycles, respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The SEI composition was analyzed collecting XPS data of the Bi@NC and NC electrodes after 10 cycles at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0003">3b</a> and Figure S26). As shown in F 1s spectra, two peaks at 684.4 and 688.3 eV are assigned to Na−F and P−F, respectively, which demonstrates a larger NaF content of the Bi@NC for a more stable NaF-rich SEI.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0057_R_d39831919e931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a></sup></span> Additionally, the C 1s spectrum is deconvoluted into three peaks at 285.2, 286.6, and 289.5 eV indicating C−C, C−O, and ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na, respectively. This C 1s spectrum confirms the presence of organic components in SEI, arising from the decomposition products of diglyme.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0005_R_d39831919e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a>, <a href="#anie202417930-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0058_R_d39831919e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27">27</a></sup></span> The lower organic content and larger NaF content of Bi@NC are attributed to its superior interfacial stability,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0061_R_d39831919e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></sup></span> contributing to its enhanced cyclability and reversibility of SMBs. Cryo-TEM were further employed to elucidate the structure of the SEI layer. Our observations reveal a uniform and dense SEI layer on the exterior of Bi@NC (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0003">3c</a>), which was enriched with various inorganic components, including NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0062_R_d39831919e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29">29</a></sup></span> These findings are aligned with the above XPS analysis. The compact nature of the SEI layer prevents the Bi@NC from further side reactions, maintaining a uniform and stable SEI layer upon cycling. By contrast, the uneven SEI layer is exposed on the surface of NC (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0003">3d</a>), which accelerates the deterioration of the SEI layer and the growth of Na dendrites for long-term cycling performance.</p>
         
         <p>In order to clarify the guiding role of sodiophilic Bi nanoparticles for the Bi@NC during the Na metal plating process, real-time Na metal plating and stripping processes were monitored by <i>in-situ</i> TEM. The experimental setup was established configuring a working electrode of Bi@NC or NC, a counter electrode of Na metal, and a solid electrolyte of Na<sub>2</sub>O (Figure S27). Upon their physical contact, a tungsten tip was subjected to a bias voltage of 1 V to initiate the Na plating process, then a deposition process was observed within 45 seconds (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0004">4a</a>). The Na front delineated by white dash lines was monitored during a Na plating and striping process. Upon an applied voltage of −1 V on the Na metal counter electrode, the Na stripping occurred immediately for the Bi@NC, and it only takes 28 seconds to occur the entire stripping process (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0004">4b</a>). On the other hand, the Na plating/stripping kinetics for the NC sample were much slower, requiring approximately 780 seconds and 1080 seconds, respectively, longer than those for the Bi@NC (Figures <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0004">4c</a>–<a href="#anie202417930-fig-0004">4d</a>). Further investigation under a higher plating voltage of 3 V revealed that the current densities for Bi@NC and NC were substantially different, and they were calculated to be 14 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively, for 1 V, as well as 30 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively, for 3 V (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0004">4e</a>, detailed methodology is provided in the Supporting Information). The visible Na plating on the outside of the NC fiber is indicative of external Na plating in NC (Figure S28), leading to deteriorate electrochemical performance due to the uneven Na plating. In stark contrast, no Na plating was observed on the surface of Bi@NC even at the high current density of 30 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, manifesting the effective regulation of Na deposition by Bi nanoparticles. The relationship between the plating length (L) and the plating time (t) was analyzed<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0065_R_d39831919e991" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Energy">30</a></sup></span> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0004">4f</a>), displaying a linear relationship between L<sup>2</sup> and t. The faster plating/stripping process under a higher plating voltage indicates that high voltage is conducive to the rapid charge transfer kinetics. According to the relation D=L<sup>2</sup>/4 t, the Na ion diffusion coefficients (D) for Bi@NC and NC at a plating voltage of 3 V were determined to be 1.26×10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> and 9.26×10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, revealing a much faster Na ion diffusion kinetics of the former. The corresponding Na deposition rate of the Bi@NC was estimated as 2.49×10<sup>6</sup> nm<sup>3</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0004">4g</a>), faster than 1.64×10<sup>5</sup> nm<sup>3</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> of the NC fiber. The significantly facilitated diffusion dynamics, as well as the sodiophilicity of the Bi nanoparticles within the void spaces of Bi@NC, contributes to the excellent Na plating/stripping behaviors even under conditions of extreme high current density.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417930-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/26b313d8-633b-4656-b70b-87a8a91fa7ef/anie202417930-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/26b313d8-633b-4656-b70b-87a8a91fa7ef/anie202417930-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/13961969-d39a-48ed-8b6d-76429dcd73e1/anie202417930-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/26b313d8-633b-4656-b70b-87a8a91fa7ef/anie202417930-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417930-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417930" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p><i>In-situ</i> TEM images showing Na plating/stripping process. TEM snapshots showing the Na plating/stripping process in Bi@NC (a, b) and NC (c, d) fiber with a plating/stripping voltage of 1/−1 V. (e) The current density at different plating voltages (1 V, 3 V). (f) The relationship between the plating L<sup>2</sup> (length) and the plating time of Bi@NC and NC fiber. (g) The rate of Na deposition within the Bi@NC and NC fiber.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To gain deeper insights into the operation mechanisms of Bi nanoparticles within the Bi@NC electrode, the phase evolution of Bi was captured conducting <i>in-situ</i> XRD analysis<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0066_R_d39831919e1062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a></sup></span> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0005">5a</a>). During the discharging process, Na−Bi alloy was formed. The characteristic peak of Bi at 27.2° corresponding to the (012) plane was gradually diminished and eventually disappeared. Simultaneously, two distinctive peaks of NaBi at 25.5° and 31.6°, corresponding to the (100) and (101) planes, appeared as Na reacted with Bi to form NaBi. As the discharge progressed, the NaBi peak was gradually weakened, while new peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi at 32.9° and 33.7°, corresponding to the (110) and (013) planes, appeared and intensified, indicating the complete transformation of NaBi into Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. During the subsequent charge process, two obvious dealloying stages were observed. Initially, the peak intensities of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi were diminished, while the peaks of NaBi appeared through the dealloying pathway. Subsequently, as further charged, the transition from NaBi back to Bi became apparent and Bi was regenerated. During a repeated discharge/charge process, the peak positions and intensities of Bi, NaBi, and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi are almost consistent confirming the remarkable reversibility of the Bi@NC electrode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0069_R_d39831919e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a></sup></span> The formation of the sodiophilic Na−Bi alloy during the electrochemical process is associated with the underlying mechanism about the stable Na deposition on the Bi@NC electrode. Furthermore, the alloying/dealloying mechanism of Bi in SMBs is described through the two alloying processes (Figure S29): Bi+Na<sup>+</sup>+e<sup>−</sup><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/fae0c639-0950-4f4e-9f8c-0f281ac8016a/anie202417930-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
NaBi, NaBi+2Na<sup>+</sup>+2e<sup>−</sup><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c240871d-53cf-4620-a53d-1f9904deaa98/anie202417930-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
Na<sub>3</sub>Bi.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0070_R_d39831919e1115" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano">33</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417930-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/245ab4d8-622a-4c99-854e-46d59cc6468a/anie202417930-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/245ab4d8-622a-4c99-854e-46d59cc6468a/anie202417930-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b899b5fd-0353-45ac-afb9-b2b37bc93537/anie202417930-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/245ab4d8-622a-4c99-854e-46d59cc6468a/anie202417930-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417930-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417930" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) The discharge—charge—discharge—charge profiles, corresponding Contour plot and line plot of the <i>in-situ</i> XRD results of the Bi@NC electrode. b) AIMD simulation snapshots, c) the corresponding RDFs, and d) the plots of t vs MSD of Na<sup>+</sup> in Na−Bi, Na<sub>2</sub>-Bi, and Na<sub>3</sub>-Bi. e) The COMSOL simulation of the local current density distribution on Cu, NC and Bi@NC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In order to further verify the “sodiophilic” of Bi and Na metal atoms diffusion kinetics within the Na<sub>x</sub>Bi systems, AIMD simulations were performed as illustrated in Figures <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0005">5b</a>–<a href="#anie202417930-fig-0005">5d</a>. As shown in the AIMD simulation images for the Na<sub>3</sub>-Bi, Na<sub>2</sub>-Bi, and Na−Bi systems (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0005">5b</a>), Na atoms diffusion routes from 0 ps to 250 ps and Bi atoms position at 250 ps are presented in one image. Na and Bi atoms are quickly diffused into each system and an open 3D diffusion trajectory of Na and Bi atoms is constructed, thus corroborating the “sodiophilic” of Bi and fast diffusion kinetics of Na atoms. Afterwards, the radial distribution functions (RDFs) and the corresponding coordination numbers for Na<sub>3</sub>-Bi, Na<sub>2</sub>-Bi and Na−Bi were determined from the last 100 ps of the simulation trajectory, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0005">5c</a>. The pronounced peaks at 3.2 Å in the RDFs indicate close proximity and stable coordination between Na and Bi atoms within the Na−Bi alloys. Bi atom prefers to bind with one Na atom for Na<sub>2</sub>-Bi and Na−Bi systems, while Bi atom with three Na atoms for Na<sub>3</sub>-Bi system, thus indicating the formation of NaBi and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi alloy during Na deposition process, which is in well-line with above <i>in-situ</i> XRD results. Subsequently, Na ion diffusion kinetics within the Na<sub>3</sub>-Bi, Na<sub>2</sub>-Bi and Na−Bi systems were determined via the Mean Square Displacement (MSD) analysis as evidenced in Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0005">5d</a>. The diffusion coefficients for Na<sup>+</sup> in Na<sub>3</sub>-Bi, Na<sub>2</sub>-Bi and Na−Bi are calculated to be 7.0×10<sup>−4</sup>, 5.0×10<sup>−4</sup>, and 3.3×10<sup>−4</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. These values are much higher than those observed for amorphous carbon (10<sup>−7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>),<span><sup><a href="#anie202417930-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417930-bib-0071_R_d39831919e1215" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Omega">34</a></sup></span> highlighting the enhanced mobility and lower resistance to Na ion diffusion of the Na−Bi alloys. The MSD analysis compellingly demonstrates that the Na−Bi alloys offer an exceptional open diffusion pathway for Na ions, which is markedly superior to that of amorphous carbon. This enhanced mobility is pivotal for achieving high-rate performance of Bi@NC electrode, as the dynamically formed Na−Bi alloy allows for rapid Na ion transport even at elevated current densities. Additionally, the current density distribution at the electrode surfaces was modeled by employing finite element simulation via COMSOL Multiphysics (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0005">5e</a>). The Cu substrate exhibited a high current density with nonuniform electric field distribution, a condition conducive to pronounced dendrite growth. By contrast, NC substrate showed moderate improvement in current distribution uniformity. Strikingly, the Bi@NC substrate displayed a lower current density coupled with pronounced charge regulation, indicating the capability of Bi particles to orchestrate uniform Na deposition on the Bi@NC. The Bi@NC electrode has been successfully designed to suppress Na dendrite formation and to confine Na metal within the void spaces of the Bi@NC fiber, which is vital for sustainable long-term cycling.</p>
         
         <p>A full battery of SMB, combining a Na@Bi@NC anode with an NVP@C cathode at a loading of 3.2 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and an N/P ratio of 4.9, was assembled to demonstrate the practical applications of Bi@NC electrode (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0006">6a</a>). The Na@Bi@NC||NVP@C full cell delivered the charge/discharge capacities of 96.50 and 95.50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure S30), effectively lighting a red LED for a practical demonstration (inset in Figure S30). After 800 cycles, the cell still maintained a high capacity of 90.45 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an impressive CE of 99.64 % (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0006">6b</a>), outperforming the inferior long-term capacity of Na@NC||NVP@C full cell (Figure S31). The NVP@C cathode in a half-cell configuration delivered a Na storage capacity of 97.58 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S32). The Na@Bi@NC||NVP@C achieved the high capacities of 99.20, 93.40, 85.20, 78.80, and 67.80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figures <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0006">6c</a> and <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0006">6d</a>). These values of Na@Bi@NC||NVP@C were much higher than those of Na@NC||NVP@C at these rates (Figure S33). <i>In-situ</i> EIS tracked the interfacial evolution in full cells and revealed that the Na@Bi@NC||NVP@C cell maintained good reversibility with low <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0006">6e</a>), indicating fast charge transfer kinetics. On the other hand, Na@NC||NVP@C cell showed a marked increase in <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, pointing to irreversible SEI deterioration and dendrite issues (Figure S34 and Table S4). The flexible Bi@NC anode can be assembled Na@Bi@NC||NVP@C pouch cells, which can successfully light on a red LED lamp under 0°, 90°, and 180° bending degree and further demonstrate prominent mechanical stability of the Bi@NC for flexible SMB applications (Figure <a href="#anie202417930-fig-0006">6f</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417930-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/186310e4-7561-4c2a-8a07-c3009145eef5/anie202417930-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/186310e4-7561-4c2a-8a07-c3009145eef5/anie202417930-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05bfb472-9a59-40ff-8d15-cfb593c0b735/anie202417930-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/186310e4-7561-4c2a-8a07-c3009145eef5/anie202417930-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417930-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417930" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Schematic illustration of the Na@Bi@NC||NVP@C full cell. b) Cycling performance and the related CE at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, c) capacity retentions and d) GCD curves at the current densities from 100 to 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and e) <i>In-situ</i> EIS results of the Na@Bi@NC||NVP@C full cells. f) Illuminating test of flexible Na@Bi@NC||NVP@C pouch cells under various folded states.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417930-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417930-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the 3D Bi@NC with a core–shell structure was successfully prepared by the electrospinning technique and has been effectively integrated into the SMA. <i>Ex-situ</i> SEM and <i>in-situ</i> optical microscopy showcase a smooth and dendrites-free electrode surface. Further, <i>in-situ</i> TEM, <i>in-situ</i> XRD and simulations confirm the “sodiophilic” Na<sub>3</sub>Bi alloy within the void space of Bi@NC facilitates rapid nucleation and ion diffusion, enabling uniform Na plating within its porous structure even at a harsh current density of 30 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. On the other hand, XPS and Cryo-TEM confirm the presence of a resilient SEI layer enveloping the amorphous carbon fibers within the Bi@NC structure. This SEI layer, bolstered by the amorphous carbon shell's capacity to buffer against substantial volume fluctuations during the plating and stripping processes, effectively suppresses side reactions, enhancing the cycle stability. As expected, the Bi@NC electrode exhibits prominent cycle life with the high CE of 99.99 % after 1000 cycles at 3 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Furthermore, the symmetric cell retains a stable voltage about 1000 h with the current density 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and superior rate performance of over 600 cycles at 30 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Finally, a full battery assembled with an NVP@C cathode and Na@ Bi/NC anode could deliver a high reversible capacity of 90.45 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 800 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Taken all together, the current work rationally proposes a 3D core–shell Bi@NC host for high performance SMBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
